## Advances in Financial Machine Learning - 0014

3.1 Motivation In Chapter 2 we discussed how to produce a matrix X of financial features out of an unstructured data set. Unsupervised learning algorithms can learn the patterns from that matrix X. For example, whether it contains hierarchical clusters, on the other hand, supervised learning algorithms require that the rows in X are associated with an array of labels or values Y, so that those labels or values can be predicted on unseen features samples. In this chapter, we will discuss ways to label financial data. 3.2 The Fixed Time Horizon Method As it relates to finance, virtually all ML papers label observations using the Fixed Time Horizon Method. This method can be described as follows. Consider a Features Matrix X with I-Rose. The Value of X sub I, sub I equals 1 to I. Drawing from some bars with index T equals 1 to T, where I is less than or equal to T. Chapter 2, Section 2.5, discussed sampling methods that produce the set of features, the value of X sub I, sub I equals 1 through I. An observation X sub I is assigned a label Y sub I is an element of the values of negative 1, 0, 1 is in equation 30 in the bonus PDF, where tau is a predefined constant threshold T sub I sub 0 is the index of the bar immediately after X sub I takes place. T sub I sub 0 plus H is the index of the H bar after T sub I sub 0 and R sub T sub I sub 0 sub T sub I sub 0 plus H is the price return over a bar horizon H, which is seen in equation 31 in the bonus PDF. Because the literature almost always works with time bars, H implies a fixed time horizon. Dixon et al. 2016 is a recent example of this labeling method. Despite its popularity, there are several reasons to avoid this approach in most cases. First, as we heard in Chapter 2, time bars do not exhibit good statistical properties. Second, the same threshold tau is applied regardless of the observed volatility. Suppose that tau equals 1 E minus 2, where sometimes we label an observation as Y sub I equals 1 subject to a realized bar volatility of sigma sub I sub 0 equals 1 E minus 4, for example, during the night session. And sometimes sigma sub T sub I sub 0 equals 1 E minus 2, for example, around the open. The large majority of labels will be 0, even if return R sub T sub I sub 0 sub T sub I sub 0 plus H was predictable and statistically significant. In other words, it is a very common error to label observations according to fixed threshold on time bars. Here are a couple of better alternatives. First, label per a varying threshold sigma sub T sub I sub 0, estimated using a rolling exponentially weighted standard deviation of returns. Second, use volume or dollar bars, as their volatilities are much closer to constant, homoscedacity. But even these two improvements, mis-a-key flaw of the fixed time horizon method, the path followed by prices. Every investment strategy has stopped loss limits, whether they are self-imposed by the portfolio manager, enforced by the risk department, or triggered by a margin call. It is simply unrealistic to build a strategy that profits from positions that would have been stopped out by the exchange. That virtually no publication accounts for that when labeling observations tells you something about the current state of the investment literature. 3.3 Computing Dynamic Thresholds. As argued in the previous section, in practice, we want to set profit taking and stop loss limits that are a function of the risks involved in a bet. Otherwise, sometimes we will be aiming too high and sometimes too low, considering the prevailing volatility. Snip at 3.1 in the bonus PDF, computes the daily volatility at intra-day estimated points, applying a span of span zero days to an exponentially weighted moving standard deviation. See the Pandas documentation for details on the pandas.series.eduM function. We can use the output of this function to set default profit taking and stop loss limits throughout the rest of this chapter. 3.4 The Triple Barrier Method. Here I will introduce an alternative labeling method that I have not found in the literature. If you are an investment professional, I think you will agree that it makes more sense. I call it the triple barrier method because it labels an observation according to the first barrier touched out of three barriers. First, we set two horizontal barriers and one vertical barrier. The two horizontal barriers are defined by profit taking and stop loss limits, which are a dynamic function of estimated volatility, whether realized or implied. The third barrier is defined in terms of number of bars elapsed since the position was taken, an expiration limit. If the upper barrier is touched first, we label the observation as a one. If the lower barrier is touched first, we label the observation as a negative one. If the vertical barrier is touched first, we have two choices, the sign of the return or a zero. I personally prefer the former as a matter of realizing a profit or loss within limits, but you should explore whether a zero works better in your particular problems. You may have noticed that the triple barrier method is path dependent in order to label an observation. We must take into account the entire path spanning t sub i sub zero, t sub i sub zero plus h, where h defines the vertical barrier, the expiration limit. We will denote t sub i sub one, the time of the first barrier touch, and the return associated with the observed feature is r sub t sub i sub zero sub t sub i sub one. For the sake of clarity, t sub i sub one is less than or equal to t sub i sub zero plus h, and the horizontal barriers are not necessarily symmetric. Snip it 3.2 in the bonus PDF, implements the triple barrier method, the function receives four arguments, close, appendus series of prices, events, appendus data frame with columns, t one, the time stamp of vertical barrier, when the value is np.nan, there will not be a vertical barrier, t r gt, the unit width of the horizontal barriers, pts one, a list of two non-negative float values, pts one index zero, the factor that multiplies t r gt to set the width of the upper barrier, if zero, there will not be an upper barrier, pts one index one, the factor that multiplies t r gt to set the width of the lower barrier, if zero, there will not be a lower barrier, molecule, a list with the subset of event indices that will be processed by a single thread, its use will become clear later on in the chapter. The output from this function

---

## Advances in Financial Machine Learning - 0015

function is a pandas data frame containing the timestamps, if any, at which each barrier was touched. As you can determine from the previous description, the method considers the possibility that each of the three barriers may be disabled. Let us denote a barrier configuration by the triplet, pt, sl, t1, where a zero means that the barrier is inactive, and one means that the barrier is active. The possible eight configurations are three useful configurations. One, one, one. This is the standard setup, where we define three barrier exit conditions. We would like to realize a profit, but we have a maximum tolerance for losses and a holding period. Zero, one, one. In this setup, we would like to exit after a number of bars, unless we are stopped out. One, one, zero. Here we would like to take a profit, as long as we are not stopped out. This is somewhat unrealistic in that we are willing to hold the position for as long as it takes. Three less realistic configurations. Zero, zero, one. This is equivalent to the fixed time horizon method. It may still be useful when applied to volume, dollar, or information driven bars, and multiple forecasts are updated within the horizon. One, zero, one. A position is held until a profit is made, or the maximum holding period is exceeded, without regard for the intermediate unrealized losses. One, zero, zero. A position is held until a profit is made. It could mean being locked on a losing position for years. Two illogical configurations. Zero, one, zero. This is an aimless configuration, where we hold the position until we are stopped out. Zero, zero, zero. There are no barriers. The position is locked forever, and no label is generated. Figure 3.1 in the bonus PDF shows two alternative configurations of the triple barrier method. On the left, the configuration is one, one, zero, where the first barrier touched is the lower horizontal one. On the right, the configuration is one, one, one, where the first barrier touched is the vertical one. Three point five. Learning side and size. In this section, we will discuss how to label examples so that an ML algorithm can learn both the side and the size of a bet. We are interested in learning the side of a bet. And we do not have an underlying model to set the side of our position long or short. Under such circumstance, we cannot differentiate between a profit taking barrier and a stop-loss barrier, since that requires knowledge of the side. Learning the side implies that either there are no horizontal barriers or that the horizontal barriers must be symmetric. In the bonus PDF, implement the function get events, which finds the time of the first barrier touch. The function receives the following arguments. Close. A panda's series of prices. T events. The panda's time index, containing the timestamps that will seed every triple barrier. These are the timestamps selected by the sampling procedures discussed in Chapter 2, Section 2.5. PTSL. A non-negative float that sets the width of the two barriers. A zero-value means that the respective horizontal barrier, profit taking and or stop-loss, will be disabled. T1. A panda's series with the timestamps of the vertical barriers. We pass a false when we want to disable vertical barriers. TRGT. A panda's series of targets expressed in terms of absolute returns. Min ret. The minimum target return required for running a triple barrier search. NUM THREADS. The number of threads concurrently used by the function. Suppose that i equals 1e6 and h equals 1e3, then the number of conditions to evaluate is up to 1 billion on a single instrument. Many ML tasks are computationally expensive unless you are familiar with multi-threading, and this is one of them. Here is where parallel computing comes into play. Chapter 20 discusses a few multi-processing functions that we will use throughout the book. Function MP panda's object calls a multi-processing engine, which is explained in depth in Chapter 20. For the moment, you simply need to know that this function will execute apply PT SL on T1 in parallel. Function apply PT SL on T1 returns the time stamps at which each barrier is touched if any. Then, the time of the first touch is the earliest time among the three returned by apply PT SL on T1. As we must learn the side of the bet, we have passed PT SL equals PT SL, PT SL, as an argument, and we arbitrarily set the side to be always long. The horizontal barriers are symmetric, so the side is irrelevant to determining the time of the first touch. The output from this function is a panda's data frame with columns. T1, the time stamp at which the first barrier is touched. TRGT, the target that was used to generate the horizontal barriers. Snipit 3.4 in the bonus PDF shows one way to define a vertical barrier. For each index in T events, it finds the time stamp of the next price bar at or immediately after a number of days, num days. This vertical barrier can be passed as an optional argument T1 in get events. Finally, we can label the observations using the get bins function defined in Snipit 3.5. The arguments are the events data frame we just discussed, and the close panda's series of prices. The output is a data frame with columns. RET, the return realized at the time of the first touched barrier. BIN, the label, the value of negative one zero one, as a function of the sign of the outcome. The function can be easily adjusted to label as zero those events when the vertical barrier was touched first, which we leave as an exercise. 3.6, meta labeling. Suppose that you have a model for setting the side of the bet long or short. You just need to learn the size of that bet, which includes the possibility of no bet at all zero size. This is a situation that practitioners face regularly. We often know whether we want to buy or sell a product, and the only remaining question is how much money we should risk in such a bet. We do not want the ML algorithm to learn the side just to tell us what is the appropriate size. At this point, it probably does not surprise you to hear that no book or paper has so far discussed this common problem. Thankfully, that misery ends here. I call this problem meta labeling because we want to build a secondary ML model that learns how to use a primary exogenous model. Rather than writing an entirely new get events function, we will make some adjustments to the previous code in order to handle meta labeling. First, we accept a new side optional argument with default none, which contains the side of our bets as decided by the primary model. Inside is not none. The function understands that meta labeling is in play. Second, because now we know the side, we can effectively discriminate between profit taking and stop loss.

## Advances in Financial Machine Learning - 0016

barriers do not need to be symmetric, as in section 3.5. Argument PTSL is a list of two non-negative float values where PTSL index zero is the factor that multiplies TRGT to set the width of the upper barrier, and PTSL index 1 is the factor that multiplies TRGT to set the width of the lower barrier. When either is zero, the respective barrier is disabled. Snip at 3.6 in the bonus PDF implements these enhancements. Likewise we need to expand the get bins function so that it handles meta labeling. Snip at 3.7 in the bonus PDF implements the necessary changes. Now the possible values for labels in out bin are zero, one, as opposed to the previous feasible values negative one, zero, one. The ML algorithm will be trained to decide whether to take the bet or pass a purely binary prediction. When the predicted label is one, we can use the probability of this secondary prediction to derive the size of the bet, where the side, sine, of the position has been set by the primary model. 3.7 How to use meta labeling Binary classification problems present a trade-off between type one errors, false positives, and type two errors, false negatives. In general, increasing the true positive rate of a binary classifier will tend to increase its false positive rate. The receiver operating characteristic ROC curve of a binary classifier measures the cost of increasing the true positive rate in terms of accepting higher false positive rates. Here 3.2 in the bonus PDF illustrates the so-called confusion matrix. On a set of observations, there are items that exhibit a condition, positives, left rectangle, and items that do not exhibit a condition, negative, right rectangle. A binary classifier predicts that some items exhibit the condition, ellipse, where the Tp area contains the true positives and the Tn area contains the true negatives. This leads to two kinds of errors, false positives, Fp, and false negatives, Fn. Precision is the ratio between the Tp area and the area in the ellipse. The Tp area is the ratio between the Tp area and the area in the left rectangle. This notion of recall, also known as true positive rate, is in the context of classification problems. The analogous to power in the context of hypothesis testing. Decreasing accuracy is the sum of the Tp and Tn areas divided by the overall set of items, square. In general, decreasing the Fp area comes at a cost of increasing the Fn area because higher precision typically means fewer calls, hence lower recall. Since there is some combination of precision and recall that maximizes the overall efficiency of the classifier, the F1 score measures the efficiency of a classifier as the harmonic average between precision and recall. More on this, in Chapter 14. Metal labeling is particularly helpful when you want to achieve higher F1 scores. First, we build a model that achieves high recall, even if the precision is not particularly high. Second, we correct for the low precision by applying metal labeling to the positives predicted by the primary model. Metal labeling will increase your F1 score by filtering out the false positives where the majority of positives have already been identified by the primary model. Stated differently, the role of the secondary ML algorithm is to determine whether a positive from the primary exogenous model is true or false. It is not its purpose to come up with a betting opportunity. Its purpose is to determine whether we should act or pass on the opportunity that has been presented. Metal labeling is a very powerful tool to have in your arsenal for four additional reasons. First, ML algorithms are often criticized as black boxes, referred to Chapter 1. Metal labeling allows you to build an ML system on top of a white box like a fundamental model founded on economic theory. This ability to transform a fundamental model into an ML model should make metal labeling particularly useful to quantum mental firms. Second. The effects of overfitting are limited when you apply metal labeling because ML will not decide the side of your bet, only the size. Third. By decoupling the side prediction from the size prediction, metal labeling enables sophisticated strategy structures. For instance, consider that the features driving a rally may differ from the features driving a sell-off. In that case, you may want to develop an ML strategy exclusively for long positions, based on the buy recommendations of a primary model and an ML strategy exclusively for short positions, based on the sell recommendations of an entirely different primary model. Fourth. Achieving high accuracy on small bets and low accuracy on large bets will ruin you, as important as identifying good opportunities is to size them properly, so it makes sense to develop an ML algorithm solely focused on getting that critical decision sizing right. We will retake this fourth point in Chapter 10. In my experience, metal labeling ML models can deliver more robust and reliable outcomes than standard labeling models. 3.8 The Quantum Mental Way You may have heard in the press that many hedge funds are embracing the quantum mental approach. A simple Google search will show reports that many hedge funds, including some of the most traditional ones, are investing tens of millions of dollars in technologies designed to combine human expertise with quantitative methods. It turns out metal labeling is exactly what these people have been waiting for. Let us hear why. Suppose that you have a series of features that you believe can forecast some prices. You just do not know how. Since you do not have a model to determine the side of each bet, you need to learn both side and sides. You apply what you have learned in Section 3.5 and produce some labels based on the triple barrier method with symmetric horizontal barriers. Now you are ready to fit your algorithm on a training set and evaluate the accuracy of your forecasts on a testing set. Alternatively, you could do the following. 1. Use your forecasts from the primary model and generate metal labels. Remember, horizontal barriers do not need to be symmetric in this case. 2. Fit your model again on the same training set, but this time using the metal labels you just generated. 3. Combine the sides from the first ML model with the sizes from the second ML model. You can always add a metal labeling layer to any primary model, whether that is an ML algorithm and econometric equation, a technical trading rule, a fundamental analysis, etc. That includes forecasts generated by a human solely based on his intuition. In that case, metal labeling will help us figure out when we should pursue or dismiss a discretionary PM's call. The features used by such metal labeling ML algorithm could range from market information to biometric statistics to psychological assessments. For example, the metal labeling ML algorithm could find that discretionary PM's tend to make particularly good calls when there is a structural break. After 17, as they may be quicker to grasp a change in the market regime.

Conversely, it may find that PMs under stress, as evidenced by fewer hours of sleep, fatigue, change in weight, etc., tend to make inaccurate predictions. Many professions require regular psychological exams, and an ML-metallabling algorithm may find that those scores are also relevant to assess our current degree of confidence on PMs' predictions. Perhaps none of these factors affect discretionary PMs, and their brains operate independently from their emotional being, like cold calculating machines. My guess is that this is not the case, and therefore meta-labeling should become an essential ML technique for every discretionary hedge fund. In the near future, every discretionary hedge fund will become a quantum firm, and meta-labeling offers them a clear path to make that transition. 3.9. Dropping unnecessary labels Some ML classifiers do not perform well when classes are too imbalanced. In those circumstances, it is preferable to drop extremely rare labels and focus on the more common outcomes. Snipit 3.8, in the bonus PDF, presents a procedure that recursively drops observations associated with extremely rare labels. Function drop labels recursively eliminates those observations associated with classes that appear less than a fraction min-PCT of cases, unless there are only two classes left. Incidentally, another reason you may want to drop unnecessary labels is this known SK Learn bug. HTTPS, colon slash slash github.com slash scikit-learn slash scikit-learn slash issues slash 8566. This sort of bug is a consequence of very fundamental assumptions in SK Learn Implementation, and resolving them as far from trivial. In this particular instance, the errors stems from SK Learn's decision to operate with standard numpy arrays rather than structured arrays or pandas objects. It is unlikely that there will be a fix by the time you are listening to this chapter or in the near future. In later chapters, we will study ways to circumvent these sorts of implementation errors by building your own classes and expanding SK Learn's functionality. 3.1 Form dollar bars for e-mini S&P 500 futures Apply a symmetric Q-sum filter, chapter 2, section 2.5.2.1, where the threshold is the standard deviation of daily returns, snippet 3.1 in the bonus PDF. What are the precision, recall, and F1 scores? 4.1, motivation. Chapter 3 presented several new methods for labeling financial observations. We introduced two novel concepts, the triple barrier method, and meta labeling, and explained how they are useful in financial applications, including quantum mental investment strategies. In this chapter, you will learn how to use sample weights to address another problem ubiquitous in financial applications. Namely, that observations are not generated by independent and identically distributed IID processes. Most of the ML literature is based on the IID assumption, and one reason many ML applications fail in finance is because those assumptions are unrealistic in the case of financial time series. 4.2, overlapping outcomes. In chapter 3, we assigned a label Y sub I to an observed feature X sub I, where Y sub I was a function of price bars that occurred over an interval. When certain conditions are met, then Y sub I and Y sub J will both depend on a common return, that is, the return over a specific interval. The implication is that the series of labels, the value of Y sub I, sub I equals 1 through I, prime, are not IID whenever there is an overlap between any two consecutive outcomes. Existential quantification of I or T sub I sub 1 is greater than T sub I plus 1 sub 0. Suppose that we circumvent this problem by restricting the bet horizon to T sub I sub 1 is less than or equal to T sub I plus 1 sub 0. In this case, there is no overlap because every feature outcome is determined before or at the onset of the next observed feature. That would lead to course models where the features sampling frequency would be limited by the horizon used to determine the outcome. On one hand, if we wish to investigate outcomes that lasted a month, features would have to be sampled with a frequency up to monthly. On the other hand, if we increased the sampling frequency to, let's say, daily, we would be forced to reduce the outcomes horizon to one day.

## Advances in Financial Machine Learning - 0018

Furthermore, if we wished to apply a path-dependent labeling technique like the triple barrier method, the sampling frequency would be subordinated to the first barrier's touch, no matter what you do, restricting the outcomes horizon to eliminate overlaps is a terrible solution. We must allow T sub i sub 1 is greater than T sub i plus 1 sub 0, which brings us back to the problem of overlapping outcomes described earlier. This situation is characteristic of financial applications. Most non-financial ML researchers can assume that observations are drawn from IID processes. For example, you can obtain blood samples from a large number of patients and measure their cholesterol. Of course, various underlying common factors will shift the mean and standard deviation of the cholesterol distribution, but the samples are still independent. There is one observation per subject. As you take those blood samples and someone in your laboratory spills blood from each tube into the following nine tubes to their right. That is, tube 10 contains blood for patient 10, but also blood from patients 1 through 9. Tube 11 contains blood from patient 11, but also blood from patients 2 through 10, and so on. Now you need to determine the features predictive of high cholesterol, diet, exercise, age, etc. without knowing for sure the cholesterol level of each patient. That is the equivalent challenge that we face in financial ML with the additional handicap that the spillage pattern is not deterministic and unknown. Intelligence is not a plug-and-play subject as it relates to ML applications. Anyone who tells you otherwise will waste your time and money. There are several ways to attack the problem of non-IID labels, and in this chapter we will address it by designing sampling and waiting schemes that correct for the undue influence of overlapping outcomes. 4.3 Number of concurrent labels Two labels y sub i and y sub j are concurrent at t when both are a function of at least one common return. Our sub t minus 1 sub t equals p sub t divided by p sub t minus 1 minus 1. The overlap does not need to be perfect in the sense of both labels spanning the same time interval. In this section we are going to compute the number of labels that are a function of a given return, our sub t minus 1 sub t. First, for each time point t equals 1 through t, we form a binary array. 4.4 Average uniqueness of a label In this section we are going to estimate a label's uniqueness non-overlap as its average uniqueness over its lifespan. First, the uniqueness of a label i at time t is u sub t sub i equals 1 sub t sub i c sub t to the negative first power. Second, the average uniqueness of label i is the average u sub t sub i over the label's lifespan. Please refer to equation 32 in the bonus PDF. This average uniqueness can also be interpreted as the reciprocal of the harmonic average of c sub t over the events lifespan. Figure 4.1 in the bonus PDF plots the histogram of uniqueness values derived from an object t1. Snip it 4.2 in the bonus PDF implements this calculation. Note that we are making use again of the function mp pandas object which speeds up calculations via multi processing. Computing the average uniqueness associated with label i u bar sub i requires information that is not available until a future time events index t1. This is not a problem because the value of u bar sub i sub i equals 1 through i are used on the training set in combination with label information and not on the testing set. These the value of u bar sub i sub i equals 1 through i are not used for forecasting the label. Hence, there is no information leakage. This procedure allows us to assign a uniqueness score between 0 and 1 for each observed feature in terms of non-overlapping outcomes. 4.5 bagging classifiers and uniqueness the probability of not selecting a particular item i after i draws with replacement on a set of i items is the value of 1 minus i to the negative first power to the i power. As the sample size grows that probability converges to the asymptopic value limit sub i through infinity. The value of 1 minus i to the negative first power to the power of i equals e to the negative first power. It means that the number of unique observations drawn is expected to be the value of 1 minus e to the negative first power is approximately equal to 2 thirds. Suppose that the maximum number of non-overlapping outcomes is k is less than or equal to i. Following the same argument, the probability of not selecting a particular item i after i draws with replacement on a set of i items is the value of 1 minus k to the negative first power to the power of i. As the sample size grows that probability can be approximated as the value of 1 minus i to the negative first power to the power of i k over i is approximately equal to e to the negative k over i power. That means that the number of unique observations drawn is expected to be 1 minus e to the power of negative k over 1 is less than or equal to 1 minus e to the negative first power. The implication is that incorrectly assuming i id draws leads to over sampling. When sampling with replacement bootstrap on observations with i to the negative first power, sigma to the i power sub i equals 1 to the u bar sub i power is much less than 1. It becomes increasingly likely that in-bag observations will be 1 redundant to each other and 2 very similar to out-of-bag observations. Redundancy of draws makes the bootstrap inefficient, referred to chapter 6. For example, in the case of a random forest, all trees in the forest will essentially be very similar copies of a single overfit decision tree, and because of the random sampling, it makes out-of-bag examples very similar to the in-bag ones out-of-bag accuracy will be grossly inflated. We will address this second issue in chapter 7 when we study cross-validation under non-ide observations. For the moment, let us concentrate on the first issue, namely bagging under observations where i to the negative first power, sigma to the power of i sub i equals 1 to the power of u bar sub i is much less than 1. A first solution is to drop overlapping outcomes before performing the bootstrap, because overlaps are not perfect, dropping an observation.

just because there is a partial overlap will result in an extreme loss of information. I do not advise you to follow this solution. A second and better solution is to utilize the average uniqueness. I to the negative first power, sigma to the power of i sub i equals 1 to the power of u bar sub i to reduce the undue influence of outcomes that contain redundant information. Accordingly, we could sample only a fraction out the index TW to the mean of observations or a small multiplier of that. In SK Learn, the SK Learn Unsemble bagging classifier class accepts an argument max samples, which can be set to max samples equals out the index of TW to the mean. In this way, we enforce that the in-bag observations are not sampled at a frequency much higher than their uniqueness. Random forests do not offer that max samples functionality. However, a solution is to bag a large number of decision trees. We will discuss this solution further in Chapter 6. 4.5.1 Sequential Bootstrap A third and better solution is to perform a sequential bootstrap where draws are made according to a changing probability that controls for redundancy. Row at all 1997 propose sequential resampling with replacement until K distinct original observations appear. Although interesting, their scheme does not fully apply to our financial problem. In the following sections, we introduce an alternative method that addresses directly the problem of overlapping outcomes. First, an observation x sub i is drawn from a uniform distribution. i is similar to u, 1, i. That is the probability of drawing any particular value i is originally delta sub i to the first power equals i to the negative first power. For the second draw, we wish to reduce the probability of drawing an observation x sub j with a highly overlapping outcome. Remember, a bootstrap allows sampling with repetition, so it is still possible to draw x sub i again, but we wish to reduce its likelihood since there is an overlap. In fact, a perfect overlap between x sub i and itself. Let us denote as phi the sequence of draws so far which may include repetitions. Until now, we know that phi to the first power equals the value of i. The uniqueness of j at the time t is u sub t sub j to the second power. Equals 1 sub t sub j, the value of 1 plus sigma sub k is a member of phi to the first power, 1 sub t sub k to the negative first power. As that is the uniqueness that results from adding alternative j's to the existing sequence of draws phi to the first power, the average uniqueness of j is the average u sub t sub j to the second power over j's lifespan. U bar sub j to the second power equals the value of sigma sub t to the power of t equals 1 to the power of u sub t sub j. The value of sigma to the power of t sub t equals 1 to the power of 1 sub t sub j to the negative first power. We can now make a second draw based on the updated probabilities. The value of delta sub j to the second power sub j equals 1 through i in equation 33 in the bonus PDF, where the value of delta sub j to the second power sub j equals 1 through i are scaled to add up to 1. Sigma to the power of i sub j equals 1 delta sub j to the second power equals 1. We can now do a second draw, update phi to the second power, and re-evaluate the value of delta sub j cubed sub j equals 1 through i. The process is repeated until i draws have taken place. This sequential bootstrap scheme has the advantage that overlaps even repetitions are still possible, but decreasingly likely. The sequential bootstrap sample will be much closer to i id than samples drawn from the standard bootstrap method. This can be verified by measuring an increase in i minus 1 sigma to the power of i sub i equals 1 u bar sub i relative to the standard bootstrap method. 4.5.2 Implementation of sequential bootstrap. We can evaluate the efficiency of the sequential bootstrap algorithm through experimental methods. Snip it 4.7 in the bonus PDF lists the function that generates a random t1 series for a number of observations num ob's i. Each observation is made at a random number drawn from a uniform distribution with boundaries 0 and num bars, where num bars is the number of bars t. The number of bars spanned by the observation is determined by drawing a random number from a uniform distribution with boundaries 0 and max h. Snip it 4.8 in the bonus PDF takes that random t1 series and derives the implied indicator matrix in m. This matrix is then subjected to two procedures. In the first one we derive the average uniqueness from a standard bootstrap random sampling with replacement. In the second one we derive the average uniqueness by applying our sequential bootstrap algorithm results are reported as a dictionary. These operations have to be repeated over a large number of iterations. Snip it 4.9 in the bonus PDF implements this Monte Carlo using the multi processing techniques discussed in chapter 20. For example, it will take about 6 hours for a 24 cores server to carry out a Monte Carlo of 16 iterations where num ob's equals 10 num bars equals 100 and max h equals 5. Without parallelization, a similar Monte Carlo experiment would have taken about 6 days. Figure 4.2 in the bonus PDF plots the histogram of the uniqueness from standard bootstrapped samples left and the sequentially bootstrapped samples right. The median of the average uniqueness for the standard method is 6 tenths and the median of the average uniqueness for the sequential method is 7 tenths and a nova test on the difference of means returns a vanishingly small probability. Statistically speaking, samples from the sequential bootstrap method have an expected uniqueness that exceeds that of the standard bootstrap method at any reasonable confidence level. 4.6 return attribution In the previous section we learned a method to bootstrap samples closer to IID. In this section, we will introduce a method to wait those samples for the purpose of training and ML algorithm. Highly overlapping outcomes would have disproportionate weights if considered equal to non-overlapping outcomes. At the same time, labels associated with large absolute returns should be given more importance than labels with negligible absolute returns. In short, we need to wait observations by some function of both uniqueness and absolute return. Please refer to function 1 in the bonus PDF for the method to wait an observation as a function. The rationale for this method is that we wish to wait an observation as a function of the absolute log returns that can be attributed uniquely to it. However, this method will not work if there is a neutral return below threshold case. For that case, lower returns should be assigned higher weights, not the reciprocal. The neutral case is unnecessary as it can be implied by a negative one or one prediction with low confidence. This is one of several reasons I would generally advise you to drop neutral cases. Snip it 4.10 in the bonus PDF implements this method. It is worth discussing a few interesting cases. C equals 1 means that there is no time decay. 0 less than C less than 1 means that weights decay linearly over time, but every observation still receives a strictly positive weight, regardless of how old. C equals 0 means that weights converge linearly to 0 as they become older. C less than 0 means that the oldest portion, C t, of the observations receive zero weight. For example, they are erased from memory. Figure 4.3 in the bonus PDF shows the decayed weights out the index of W times DF after applying the decay factors for C is a member of the set 1 75 100 5 10 0 negative 25 100 negative 5 10s. Although not necessarily practical, the procedure allows the possibility of generating weights that increase as they get older by setting C to greater than 1 4.8 class weights. In addition to sample weights, it is often useful to apply class weights. Class weights are weights that correct for underrepresented labels. This is particularly critical in classification problems where the most important classes have rare occurrences. King and Zeng 2001. For example, suppose that you wish to predict liquidity crisis, like the Flash Crash of May 6, 2010. These events are rare relative to the millions of observations that take place in between them. Unless we assign higher weights to the samples associated with those rare labels, the ML algorithm will maximize the accuracy of the most common labels and Flash crashes will be deemed to be outliers rather than rare events. ML libraries typically implement functionality to handle class weights. For example, SK learn penalizes errors in samples of class index J. J equals 1 through J, with waiting class weight the index J rather than 1. Accordingly, higher class weights on label J will force the algorithm to achieve higher accuracy on J. When class weights do not add up to J, the effect is equivalent to changing the regularization parameter of the classifier. In financial applications, the standard labels of a classification algorithm are a set of negative 1 and 1, where the 0 or a neutral case will be implied by a prediction with probability only slightly above 5 tenths and below some neutral threshold. There is no reason for favoring accuracy of one class over the other, and as such a good default is to assign class weight equals balanced. This choice reweights observations so as to simulate that all classes appeared with equal frequency. In the context of bagging classifiers, you may want to consider the argument class weight equals balanced sub-sample, which means that class weight equals balanced will be applied to the in-bag bootstrap samples rather than the entire data set. For a full details, it is helpful to read the source code implementing class weight in SK Learn.

Fractionally differentiated features. 5.1 Motivation It is known that as a consequence of arbitrage forces, financial series exhibit low signal to noise ratios, to make matters worse, standard stationary transformations, like integer differentiation, further reduce that signal by removing memory. Price series have memory because every value is dependent upon a long history of previous levels. In contrast, integer differentiated series, like returns, have a memory cutoff in the sense that history is disregarded entirely after a finite sample window. Once stationary transformations have wiped out all memory from the data, statisticians resort to complex mathematical techniques to extract whatever residual signal remains. Not surprisingly, applying these complex techniques on memory erased series likely leads to false discoveries. In this chapter, we introduce a data transformation method that ensures the stationarity of the data while preserving as much memory as possible. 5.2 The stationarity versus memory dilemma It is common in finance to find non-stationary time series. What makes these series non-stationary is the presence of memory. For example, a long history of previous levels that shift the series mean over time. In order to perform inferential analyses, researchers need to work with invariant processes such as returns on prices or changes in log prices, changes in yield, or changes in volatility. These data transformations make the series stationary at the expense of removing all memory from the original series. Although stationarity is a necessary property for inferential purposes, it is rarely the case in signal processing that we wish all memory to be erased, as that memory is the basis for the model's predictive power. For example, equilibrium stationary models need some memory to assess how far the price process has drifted away from the long term expected value in order to generate a forecast. The dilemma is that returns are stationary, however, memoryless, and prices have memory, however they are non-stationary. The question arises, what is the minimum amount of differentiation that makes a price series stationary while preserving as much memory as possible? Accordingly, we would like to generalize the notion of returns to consider stationary series where not all memory is erased. Under this framework, returns are just one kind of, and in most cases, sub-optimal price transformation among many other possibilities. Part of the importance of cointegration methods is their ability to model series with memory. But why would the particular case of zero differentiation deliver best outcomes? Zero differentiation is as arbitrary as one step differentiation. There is a wide region between these two extremes, fully differentiated series on one hand, and zero differentiated series on the other. That can be explored through fractional differentiation for the purpose of developing a highly predictive ML model. Supervised learning algorithms typically require stationary features. The reason is that we need to map a previously unseen unlabeled observation to a collection of labeled examples, and in fur from them, the label of that new observation. If the features are not stationary, we cannot map the new observation to a large number of known examples, but stationary does not ensure predictive power. Stationarity is a necessary, non-sufficient condition for the high performance of an ML algorithm. The problem is, there is a trade-off between stationary and memory. We can always make a series more stationary through differentiation, but it will be at the cost of erasing some memory, which will defeat the forecasting purpose of the ML algorithm. In this chapter, we will study one way to resolve this dilemma. 5.3 Literature Review Virtually all the financial time series literature is based on the premise of making non-stationary series stationary through integer transformation.

---

## Advances in Financial Machine Learning - 0022

This raises two questions. 1. Why would integer 1 differentiation, like the one used for computing returns on log prices, be optimal? 2. Is over differentiation one reason why the literature has been so biased in favor of the efficient markets hypothesis? The notion of fractional differentiation applied to the predictive time series analysis dates back at least to housing 1981. In that paper, a family of RMA processes was generalized by permitting the degree of differencing to take fractional values. This was useful because fractionality, differenced processes, exhibit long-term persistence and anti-persistence, hence enhancing the forecasting power compared to the standard RMA approach. In the same paper, Hosking states, apart from a passing reference by Granger 1978, fractional differencing does not appear to have been previously mentioned in connection with time series analysis. After Hosking's paper, the literature on this subject has been surprisingly scarce, adding up to eight journal articles written by only nine authors. Hosking, Johansson, Nielsen, McKinnon, Jensen, Jones, Papille, Cavallair, and Taylor. Most of those papers relate to technical matters, such as fast algorithms for the calculation of fractional differentiation, in continuous stochastic processes, for example, Jensen and Nielsen 2014. Differentiating the stochastic process is a computationally expensive operation. In this chapter, we will take a practical alternative and novel approach to recover stationarity. We will generalize the difference operator to non-integer steps. Please refer to sections 5.4 and 5.4.1 in the bonus PDF for the method and the series on long memory. 5.4.2, iterative estimation. Considering the sequence of weights, omega, we can appreciate that for k equals zero to infinity with omega sub zero equals one. The weights can be generated iteratively as omega sub k equals negative omega sub k d minus k plus one over k to the negative first power. Figure 5.1 in the bonus PDF plots the sequence of weights used to compute each value of the fractionally differentiated series. The legend reports the value of D used to generate each sequence. The x-axis indicates the value of k, and the y-axis shows the value of omega sub k. For example, for D equals zero, all weights are zero except for omega sub zero equals one. That is the case where the differentiated series coincides with the original one. For D equals one, all weights are zero except for omega sub zero equals one and omega sub one equals negative one. That is the standard first order integer differentiation, which is used to derive log price returns. Anywhere in between these two cases, all weights after omega sub zero equals one are negative and greater than negative one. Figure 5.2 in the bonus PDF plots the sequence of weights where D is a member of the set one, two, at increments of one-tenth. For D is greater than one, we observe omega sub one is less than negative one and omega sub k is greater than zero for all k's greater than or equal to two. Snip it 5.1 in the bonus PDF list the code used to generate these plots. 5.4.3 convergence. Let us consider the convergence of the weights. From that result, we can determine that for k greater than D, if omega sub k minus one does not equal zero, then the absolute value of omega sub k over omega sub k minus one equals the absolute value of one. So, we can determine the value of D minus k plus one over k less than one and omega sub k equals zero otherwise. Consequently, the weights converge asymptotically to zero as an infinite product of factors within the unit circle. Also, for a positive D and k less than D plus one, we have D minus k plus one over k is greater than or equal to zero, which makes the initial weights alternate inside. For a non-integer D, once k is greater than or equal to D plus one, omega sub k will be negative if the integer value of D is even and positive otherwise. Summarizing the limit of k to infinity omega sub k equals zero negative converges to zero from the left when the integer value of D is even and positive otherwise. Summarizing the limit of k to infinity omega sub k equals zero negative converges to zero from the left when the integer value of D is even and the limit of k to infinity omega sub k equals zero positive converges to zero from the right when the integer value of D is odd. In the special case of D being a member of zero one, this means that negative one less than omega sub k less than zero for all values of k greater than zero. This alternation of weight signs is necessary to make the value of x tilde sub t sub t equals one through t stationary as memory wanes or is offset over the long run. 5.5 Implementation In this section we will explore two alternative implementations of fractional differentiation, the standard expanding window method and a new method that I call fixed with window fractive ffd. 5.5.1 Expanding Window Let us discuss how to fractionally differentiate a finite time series in practice suppose a time series with T real observations, the value of x sub t t equals one through t because of data limitations, the fractionally differentiated value x tilde sub t cannot be computed on the right. 5.5 Implementation In this section we will explore two alternative implementations of fractional differentiation, the value of x tilde sub t equals zero through t minus one and tilde x sub t minus one will use weights the value of omega sub k equals zero through t minus L minus one. This means that the initial points will have a different amount of memory compared to the final points for each L we can determine the relative weight loss. 5.5 Implementation In this section we will explore two alternative implementations of fractional differentiation, the value of x tilde sub t minus one will use weights the value of x tilde sub t minus one and tilde x sub t minus one will use weights the value of x tilde sub t minus one. 5.5 Given a tolerance level, tau is a member of zero, one. This value L variables corresponds to the first result in the value of x tilde sub t sub t equals one.

Alternatively, fractional differentiation can be computed using a fixed width window that is dropping the weights after their modulus, the absolute value of omega sub K falls below a given threshold value tau. This is equivalent to finding the first L variables such that the absolute value of omega sub L variables are equal to or greater than tau, and the absolute value of omega sub L variables plus 1 are equal to or less than tau, setting a new variable omega tilde sub K. Omega tilde sub K equals omega K if K is equal to or less than L variables is equal to 0 if K is greater than L variables and X tilde sub T equals sigma to the power of L variables sub K equals 0 omega tilde sub K X sub T minus K for T equals T minus L variables plus 1 through T. Figure 5.4 in the bonus PDF plots e-mini SNP 500 futures trade bars for size 1 E4 rolled forward fractionally differentiated D equals 4 tenths tau equals 1 E minus 5. This procedure has the advantage that the same vector of weights is used across all estimates of the value of X tilde sub T, sub T equals L variables to T. Hence, avoiding the negative drift caused by an expanding window's added weights. The result is a driftless blend of level plus noise as expected. The distribution is no longer Gaussian as a result of the skewness and excess kurtosis that comes with memory, however it is stationary. SNP 5.3 in the bonus PDF presents an implementation of this idea. 5.6 stationary with maximum memory preservation. Consider a series the value of X sub T sub T equals 1 through T, applying the fixed with window for active FFD method on this series. We can compute the minimum coefficient D variables such that the resulting fractionally differentiated series, the value of X tilde sub T, sub T equals L variables to T, is stationary. This coefficient D variables quantifies the amount of memory that needs to be removed to achieve stationarity. If the value of X sub T, sub T equals L variables to T, is already stationary, then D variables equals 0. If the value of X sub T, sub T equals L variables to T, contains a unit root, then D variables are less than 1. If the value of X sub T, sub T equals L variables to T, exhibits explosive behavior like in a bubble, then D variables are greater than 1. A case of particular interest is 0 is less than D variables is much less than 1. When the original series is mildly non stationary, in this case, although the differentiation is needed, a full integer differentiation removes excessive memory and predictive power. Figure 5.5 in the bonus PDF illustrates this concept. On the right Y axis, it plots the ADF statistic computed on e-mini S-TMP 500 futures log prices rolled forward using the ETF trick, down sample to daily frequency, going back to the contract inception. On the X axis, it displays the D value used to generate this series on which the ADF statistic was computed. The original series has an ADF statistic of negative 3,387,10,000, while the returns series has an ADF statistic of negative 46 and 9,114,10,000. At a 95% confidence level, the test's critical value is negative 2 and 8,623,10,000. The ADF statistic crosses that threshold in the vicinity of D equals 35,100. The left Y axis plots the correlation between the original series D equals 0, and the differentiated series at various D values. At D equals 3500s, the correlation is still very high. At 995,000s, this confirms that the procedure introduced in this chapter has been successful in achieving stationary without giving up too much memory. In contrast, the correlation between the original series and the returns series is only 3100s. Hence, showing that the standard integer differentiation wipes out the series's memory almost entirely. Virtually all finance papers attempt to recover a stationarity by applying an integer differentiation D equals 1 that is much greater than 35100s, which means that most studies have over-differentiated the series, that is, they have removed much more memory than was necessary to satisfy standard econometric assumptions. SNP 5.4 in the bonus PDF lists the code used to produce these results. The example on e-many futures is by no means and exception. Table 5.1 in the bonus PDF shows the ADF statistics after applying FFD D on various values of D for 87 of the most liquid futures worldwide. In all cases, the standard D equals 1 used for computing.

---

## Advances in Financial Machine Learning - 0024

returns implies over differentiation. In fact, in all cases, stationarity is achieved with D being less than 6 tenths, in some cases like orange juice, J01 commodity, or live cattle, LC1 commodity, no differentiation at all was needed. 5.7 Conclusion to summarize most econometric analyses follow one of two paradigms. One, box Jenkins, returns are stationary, however, memoryless. Two, angle-granger, log prices have memory, however they are non-stationary. Cointegration is the trick that makes regression work on non-stationary series, so that memory is preserved. However, the number of cointegrated variables is limited, and the cointegrating vectors are notoriously unstable. In contrast, the FFD approach introduced in this chapter shows that there is no need to give up all of the memory in order to gain stationarity, and there is no need for the cointegration trick as it relates to ML forecasting. Once you become familiar with FFD, it will allow you to achieve stationarity without renouncing to memory or predictive power. In practice, I suggest you experiment with the following transformation of your features. First, compute a cumiled of sum of the time series. This guarantees that some order of differentiation is needed. Second, compute the FFD-D series for various values of D as a member of 0, 1. Third, determine the minimum D such that the P value of the ADF statistic on FFD-D falls below 5%. Fourth, use the FFD-D series as your predictive feature. 5.1 Generated time series from an IID-Gosian random process, this is a memoryless stationery series. A, compute the ADF statistic on this series. What is the P value? B, compute the cumiled of sum of the observations. This is a non-stationary series without memory. 1. What is the order of integration of this cumiled of series? 2. Compute the ADF statistic on this series. What is the P value? C, differentiate the series twice. What is the P value of this over-differentiated series? 5.2 Generated time series that follows a sinusoidal function, this is a stationery series with memory. A, compute the ADF statistic on this series. What is the P value? B, shift every observation by the same positive value. Compute the cumiled of sum of the observations. This is a non-stationary series with memory. 1. Compute the ADF statistic on this series. What is the P value? 2. Apply an expanding window fructive with tau equals 1 E minus 2 for what minimum D value do you get a P value below 5 percent? 3. Apply FFD with tau equals 1 E minus 5 for what minimum D value do you get a P value below 5 percent? 5.3 Take the series from exercise 2b. A, fit the series to assign function. What is the R squared? Compute fructive FFD fructive FFD series D negative D. What do you get? Why? 5.5 Take the dollar bar series on E mini S&P 500 futures. A, form a new series as a cumul of sum of log prices. C, compute the correlation of the fructive series to the original untransformed series. D, apply an angle granger cointegration test on the original and fructive series. Are they cointegrated? Why? C, form labels using the triple barrier method with symmetric horizontal barriers of twice the daily standard deviation and a vertical barrier of five days. D, fit a bagging classifier of decision entries, where 1, the observed features are bootstrapped using the sequential method from chapter 4. 2, on each bootstrapped sample, sample weights are determined using the techniques from chapter 4. 2, modeling chapter 6, ensemble methods, 6.1 motivation. In this chapter we will discuss two of the most popular ML ensemble methods, as everywhere else in this book, the assumption is that you have already used these approaches. The goal of this chapter is to explain what makes them effective and how to avoid common errors that lead to their misuse in finance. 6.2, the three sources of errors. ML models generally suffer from three errors. 1, bias. This error is caused by unrealistic assumptions. When bias is high, the ML algorithm has failed to recognize important relations between features and outcomes. In this situation, the algorithm is said to be under fit. 2, variance. This error is caused by sensitivity to small changes in the training set. When variance is high, the algorithm has overfit the training set, and that is why even minimal changes in the training set can produce wildly different predictions. Rather than modeling the general patterns in the training set, the algorithm has mistaken noise with signal. 3, noise. This error is caused by the variance of the observed values, like unpredictable changes or measurement errors. This is the irreducible error which cannot be explained by any model. 6.3, bootstrap aggregation. Bootstrap aggregation, bagging, is an effective way of reducing the variance in forecasts. It works as follows. First, generate end training data sets by random sampling with replacement. Second, fit end estimators. One on each training set. These estimators are fit independently from each other, hence the models can be fit in parallel. Third, the ensemble forecast is the simple average of the individual forecasts from the end models. In the case of categorical variables, the probability that an observation belongs to a class is given by the proportion of estimators that classify that observation as a member of that class, majority voting. When the base estimator

---

## Advances in Financial Machine Learning - 0025

can make forecasts with a prediction probability the bagging classifier may derive a mean of the probabilities. If you use SK learns bagging classifier class to compute the out of bag accuracy, you should be aware of this bug. One workaround is to rename the labels in integer sequential order. Please refer to section 6.3.1 in the bonus PDF for that specific section. 6.3.2 Improved Accuracy Consider a bagging classifier that makes a prediction on K classes by majority voting among n independent classifiers. We can label the predictions as a set of 0 and 1, where 1 means a correct prediction. The accuracy of a classifier is the probability p of labeling a prediction as 1. On average, we will get n p predictions labeled as 1, with variance n p, the value of 1 minus p. Majority voting makes the correct prediction when the most forecasted class is observed. For example, for n equals 10 and K equals 3, the bagging classifier made a correct prediction when class A was observed and the cast votes were a set of A, B, C equals 4, 3, 3. However, the bagging classifier made an incorrect prediction when class A was observed and the cast votes were a set of A, B, C equals 4, 1, 5. A sufficient condition is that the sum of these labels is x is greater than n over 2. A necessary non-sufficient condition is that x is greater than n over K, which occurs with probability in equation 35 in the bonus PDF. The implication is that for a sufficiently large n, say n is greater than p, the value of p minus 1 over K to the negative second power, then p is greater than 1 over K as it approaches p, the value of x greater than n over K greater than p. Hence, the bagging classifier's accuracy exceeds the average accuracy of the individual classifiers, snippet 6.1 in the bonus PDF, implements this calculation. This is a strong argument in favor of bagging any classifier in general when computational requirements permit it. However, unlike boosting, bagging cannot improve the accuracy of poor classifiers. If the individual learners are poor classifiers, p much less than 1 over K, majority voting will still perform poorly, although with lower variance. Figure 6.2 in the bonus PDF illustrates these facts, because it is easier to achieve row bar much less than 1 than p greater than 1 over K, bagging is more likely to be successful in reducing variance than in reducing bias. For further analysis on this topic, the listener is directed to condor-set jury theorem. Although the theorem is derived for the purposes of majority voting in political science, the problem addressed by this theorem shares similarities with the previous discussion. 6.3.3 Observation redundancy In Chapter 4, we studied one reason why financial observations cannot be assumed to be IID. Redundant observations have two detrimental effects on bagging. First, the samples drawn with replacement are more likely to be virtually identical, even if they do not share the same observations. This makes row bar similar to 1, and bagging will not reduce variance regardless of n. For example, if each observation at t is labeled according to the return between t and t plus 100, we should sample 1% of the observations per bagged estimator, but not more. Chapter 4, section 4.5, recommended three alternative solutions, one of which consisted of setting max samples equals out index TW, the value of the mean, in SK learns implementation of the bagging classifier class. Another better solution was to apply the sequential bootstrap method. The second detrimental effect from observation redundancy is that out of bag accuracy will be inflated. This happens because random sampling with replacement places in the training set samples that are very similar to those out of bag. In such a case, a proper stratified K-fold cross validation without shuffling before partitioning will show a much lower testing set accuracy than the one estimated out of bag. For this reason, it is advisable to set stratified K-fold and splits equals K-shuffle equals false when using that SK learned class, cross validate the bagging classifier and ignore the out of bag accuracy results. A low number K is preferred to a high one as excessive partitioning would again place in the testing set samples too similar to those used in the training set. 6.4, random forest. Random trees are known to be prone to overfitting, which increases the variance of the forecasts. In order to address this concern, the random forest RF method was designed to produce ensemble forecasts with lower variance. RF shares some similarities with bagging, in the sense of training independently individual estimators over bootstrapped subsets of the data. The key difference with bagging is that random forests incorporate a second level of randomness. When optimizing each node split, only a random sub-sample without replacement of the attributes will be evaluated, with the purpose of further decoralating the estimators. Like bagging, RF reduces forecasts variance without overfitting. Remember, as long as row bar is less than 1, a second advantage is that RF evaluates feature importance, which we will discuss in depth in chapter 8. A third advantage is that RF provides out of bag accuracy estimates, however in financial applications, they are likely to be inflated, as discussed in section 6.3.3. But like bagging, RF will not necessarily exhibit lower bias than individual decision trees. If a large number of samples are redundant, non-ID, overfitting will still take place. Depending randomly with replacement, will build a large number of essentially identical trees. Row bar is similar to 1, where each decision tree is overfit, a flaw for which decision trees are notorious. Unlike bagging, RF always fixes the size of the bootstrapped samples to match the size of the training data set. Let us review ways we can address this RF overfitting problem in SK Learn. For illustration purposes, I will refer to SK Learn's classes, however, these solutions can be applied to any implementation. 1. Set a parameter max features to a lower value as a way of forcing discrepancy between trees. 2. Early stopping. Set the regularization parameter, min-weight fraction-leaf, to a sufficiently large value, for example, 5%, such that out of bag accuracy converges to out of sample, k-fold accuracy. 3. Use bagging classifier on Decision Tree Classifier.

---

## Advances in Financial Machine Learning - 0026

where max samples is set to the average uniqueness average U between samples. A, CLF equals Decision Tree classifier, criterion equals entropy, max features equals auto, class weight equals balanced. B, BC equals bagging classifier, base estimator equals CLF, and estimators equals 1000, max samples equals average U, max features equals 1. 4, Use bagging classifier on random forest classifier where max samples is set to the average uniqueness average U between samples. A, CLF equals random forest classifier, and estimators equals 1 criterion equals entropy, bootstrap equals false, class weight equals balanced sub sample. B, BC equals bagging classifier, base estimator equals CLF, and estimators equals 1000, max samples equals average U, max features equals 1. 5, Modify the RF class to replace standard bootstrapping with sequential bootstrapping. In summary, snippet 6.2 in the bonus PDF demonstrates three alternative ways of setting up an RF using different classes. When fitting Decision Trees, a rotation of the features space in a direction that aligns with the axes typically reduces the number of levels needed by the tree. For this reason, I suggest you fit RF on a PCA of the features as that may speed up calculations and reduce some overfitting, more on this in Chapter 8. Also, as discussed in Chapter 4, Section 4.8, class weight equals balanced sub sample will help you prevent the trees from misclassifying minority classes. 6.5, Boosting. Currents and valiant 1989 were among the first to ask whether one could combine weak estimators in order to achieve one with high accuracy. Shortly after, Shapire, 1990, demonstrated that the answer to that question was affirmative, using the procedure we today call boosting. In general terms, it works as follows. First, generate one training set by random sampling with replacement, according to some sample weights initialized with uniform weights. Second, fit one estimator using that training set. Third, if the single estimator achieves an accuracy greater than the acceptance threshold, for example 50% in a binary classifier so that it performs better than chance, the estimator is kept. Otherwise, it is discarded. Fourth, give more weight to misclassified observations and less weight to correctly classified observations. Fifth, repeat the previous steps until n estimators are produced. Sixth, the ensemble forecast is the weighted average of the individual forecasts from the n models, where the weights are determined by the accuracy of the individual estimators. There are many boosting algorithms of which at a boost is one of the most popular, Geron 2017. Figure 6.3 in the bonus PDF summarizes the decision flow of a standard at a boost implementation. 6.6, bagging versus boosting in finance. From the previous description, a few aspects make boosting quite different from bagging. Individual classifiers are fit sequentially. Poor performing classifiers are dismissed. Observations are weighted differently in each iteration. The ensemble forecast is a weighted average of the individual learners. Boosting's main advantage is that it reduces both variance and bias in forecasts. However, correcting bias comes at the cost of greater risk of overfitting. It could be argued that in financial applications, bagging is generally preferable to boosting. Bagging addresses overfitting, while boosting addresses underfitting. Overfitting is often a greater concern than underfitting, as it is not difficult to overfit an ML algorithm to financial data because of the low signal to noise ratio. Furthermore, bagging can be parallelized while generally boosting requires sequential running. 6.7, bagging for scalability. As you know, several popular ML algorithms do not scale well with the sample size. Support vector machines, SVMs, are a prime example. If you attempt to fit an SVM on a million observations, it may take a while until the algorithm converges. And even once it has converged, there is no guarantee that the solution is a global optimum or that it is not overfit. One practical approach is to build a bagging algorithm where the base estimator belongs to a class that does not scale well with the sample size, like SVM. When defining that base estimator, we will impose a tight early stopping condition. For example, in SK learns SVM implementation, you could set a low value for the max-inter parameter, say 1E5 iterations. The default value is max-inter equals negative 1, which tells the estimator to continue performing iterations until errors fall below a tolerance level. Alternatively, you could raise the tolerance level through the parameter TOL, which has a default value TOL equals 1E-3. Either of these two parameters will force an early stop. You can stop other algorithms early with equivalent parameters, like the number of levels in an RF max-def, or the minimum weighted fraction of the sum total of weights of all the input samples required to be at a leaf node, min-weight fraction-leaf. Given that bagging algorithms can be parallelized, we are transforming a large sequential task into many smaller ones that are run simultaneously. Of course, the early stopping will increase the variance of the outputs from the individual base estimators. However, that increase can be more than offset by the variance reduction associated with the bagging algorithm. You can control that reduction by adding more independent base estimators. Used in this way, bagging will allow you to achieve fast and robust estimates on extremely large data sets. Exercises. 6.1. Why is bagging based on random sampling with replacement? Would bagging still reduce a forecast variance if sampling were without replacement? 6.2. Suppose that your training set is based on highly overlap labels, for example, with low uniqueness as defined in chapter 4. A. Does this make bagging prone to overfitting or just ineffective? Why? B. Is out of bag accuracy generally reliable in financial applications? Why? 6.3. Build an ensemble of estimators where the base estimator is a decision tree. A. Could the number of trees be too high for the number of observations available? 6.5. How is out of bag accuracy different from stratified K-fold with shuffling cross-validation accuracy?
